Question No 66 by Angela Billingham (H−0502/97)

Subject: Property in the Canary Islands

I refer to Commissioner Monti's response to my Question (H−0882/96)(1) on the Law of Tourism 7/1995 which is due to come into force in Spain in July 1997.

The impact of the law will be to restrict the provision of services in any one complex to a single entity which would therefore have a monopoly on lettings in the complex, removing the freedom available to tourists of using competing providers of letting apartments. Could the Commission investigate the possibility of this practice being pro‐monopoly and anti‐competitive?

With regard to the law's objective being to protect tourists as users of services, does the Commission not believe owners of apartments are frequent tourists who put a great deal into the local economy?

Finally, can the Commission produce any evidence that the new law will produce standards of safety, cleanliness and hygiene? Who can say that a company managing many apartments will provide higher standards of cleanliness and decoration than an individual owner who lets out his own apartment when he or she is not using it?

Answer

1. It is difficult to see how the system introduced by Law 7/1995 for the management of building complexes tends to create a dominant position. After all, the monopoly enjoyed by the body in charge of the management of a complex is confined to that complex, and so prevents that body from acquiring a dominant position in the overall market concerned, which is the tourist letting market in the Canary Islands.

Furthermore, no evidence has been produced of any agreement between these bodies, which would be contrary to the rules of competition.

2. As for the involvement of property owners in the local economy of the Canary Islands, that is undeniable. A very large proportion of that economy is focused on the tourist industry, hence the objective of Law 7/1995 of “regulating the organization and promotion of the sector as a strategic economic factor’.

These property owners will be subject to certain constraints when trying to let their properties for tourist purposes, but on the other hand, when they themselves are in residence, they will benefit from the provisions of the law as receivers of services.

3. Finally, given the typical features of accommodation in the Canary Islands, which is principally made up of apartments within large blocks, the law provides that the body responsible for the management of a building, or part of a building, will be responsible for ensuring that the standards of hygiene, cleanliness and safety (especially fire safety) are observed both within the apartments and in the communal parts of the building, something that cannot be arranged on a personal basis. If these conditions are not met, the body in question will be held professionally liable.

Furthermore, this body must provide a permanently manned reception area on the premises for which it is responsible.

However, it will not be until after 20 July, when the conditions for the application of this law come into force, that we shall be able to see how far these objectives are achieved in practice.

***

Question No 67 by Joan Colom i Naval (H−0531/97)

Subject: Entitlement of Community citizens and legal persons to locate businesses in EU Member States other than that in which they are domiciled

The tourist industry is a business that is important to the Spanish economy in general and the Catalan economy in particular. A substantial part of this business sector is owned by non‐Spanish Community citizens or companies; similarly, a significant minority of the Community workforce employed in the industry is also non‐Spanish; finally, some establishments are located close to areas of special environmental interest or required for national defence purposes.

Is there any restriction in Spain on the entitlement of non‐Spanish EU businesses or citizens to own Spanish tourist businesses? Is there any restriction on the employment of Community citizens in this sector on grounds of their nationality? Would such restrictions, if they did exist, be compatible with the acquis communautaire?

Answer

Pursuant to the provisions of Articles 52 and 58 of the EC Treaty, private individuals and companies established under the law of a Member State enjoy the right of freedom of establishment in the other Member States.

Companies may exercise this freedom by transferring their main place of activity or by setting up an agency, branch or subsidiary in another Member State. Clearly, these individuals and companies must not suffer from any discrimination on the basis of their nationality; nor must they be the subject of measures which, although applied without distinction, nevertheless obstruct the exercise of their freedom of establishment. The only measures that can be justified are those based on grounds of public order, public safety or public health, or imperative public interest considerations, proportionate to the intended objective.

As regards the existence in Spain of any restrictions on the establishment of companies or non‐Spanish nationals, the Commission has received no complaints and no information about any such restrictions.

***

Question No 68 by Jan Andersson (H−0552/97)

Subject: Analysis of the implications of abolishing tax‐free sales

At the most recent Council meeting, a majority of the Member States' Transport Ministers called for an analysis of the effects of abolishing tax‐free sales.

Will the Commission carry out such an analysis?

Answer

The Commission must remind the honourable Member that the Ecofin Council, when adopting directives abolishing frontier controls for fiscal purposes, decided to put an end to tax‐free sales with effect from 1 July 1999, but allowed for a transitional period which was to be “used to take the necessary steps to alleviate both the social impact in the sectors concerned and the regional problems arising, especially in the frontier regions’ (recital of Directive 91/680/EEC).

A possible impact of the ending of duty‐free sales on the transport sector led transport ministers to raise the question of a study at their last meeting on 18 June 1997. However, ministers were referred to a decision by finance ministers during the Ecofin Council of 11 November 1996, which had already turned down a similar request.

The Commission finds it symptomatic that the arguments put forward today (including those relating to the number of jobs in question) in favour of a study of the consequences of abolishing duty‐free sales are exactly the same as those advanced in 1991, which prompted the Commission"s decision to allow a transitional period.

The Commission therefore has no plans for carrying out such a study and can only restate its position as already described in numerous answers to parliamentary questions.

***

Question No 69 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0573/97)

Subject: Tendering for refuse collection

In a number of large Danish municipalities, the collection of household refuse is carried out by cleaning firms on the basis of long‐term contracts. In some cases, these contracts run until the years 2014 and 2020 but the work has never been put out to tender.

What is the Commission's view of the duration of these contracts in the light of the directive on the provision of services? What steps will the Commission take to enforce the provisions of that directive in Denmark?

Answer

The question posed by the honourable Member does not contain sufficient information on which to base an answer. In fact, it does not appear from the question whether the long‐term contracts for waste collection amount to service concessions contracts, or, if such is not the case, whether these contracts were awarded before the entry into force on 1 July 1993 of Council Directive 92/50/EEC of 18 June 1992 relating to the coordination of procedures for the award of public service contracts. Finally, the available information does not allow an assessment of whether the conditions for the application of the exception provided for under Article 6 of the directive may be met in certain cases. It is therefore not possible to evaluate the conformity of the cases, to which the honourable Member refers, with the provisions of the directive. If the honourable Member has additional, concrete information on the matter, she is kindly requested to forward it to the Commission in order that it may take appropriate action.

Question No 70 by Ludivina García Arias (H−0576/97)

Subject: Infringement proceedings commenced against Spain

Having issued a number of constructive and semi‐official warnings to the Spanish Government that the parliamentary group supporting it should bring the Law on Digital Television into line with Community law during its passage through Parliament (Articles 30 and 59 of the Treaty and Directive 95/47(2) ), the Commission has now rightly decided to use the exceptional urgent procedure to commence infringement proceedings and ask the Court of Justice to take protective measures. On how many occasions has the Court of Justice been asked to take such measures against a Member State? Where approved by the Court, have those measures had an immediate effect in the Member States?

Question No 71 by Manuela Frutos Gama (H−0577/97)

Subject: Digital television and decoders

What type of protective measures in relation to Spanish legislation on digital television and decoders does the Commission intend to seek from the Court of Justice?

Joint answer

The questions asked by the two honourable Members both relate to the same infringement proceedings. They raise two different issues: the Commission"s adoption of the urgent procedure in this case, and the possibility of seeking protective measures from the Court of Justice.

As to the adoption of the urgent procedure:

As the honourable Members have rightly pointed out, the urgent procedure is not normally employed in the administrative phase prior to proceedings for failure to act. In actual fact, the special feature of this procedure simply amounts to requiring the Member State to reply within a shorter period than that usually allowed by the Commission. In the case cited by the honourable Members, the Commission sent on 27 June a formal letter asking for comment, allowing the Member State fifteen calendar days within which to reply.

The Commission uses this accelerated procedure with due regard to the circumstances of each case, and its intention is always to ensure effective respect for the Treaties. In the present instance, the Spanish law that came into force on 6 May 1997 contains provisions which produce an immediate effect on trade. In particular, the Commission took due account of the two‐month period provided for in the final paragraph of Article 7(a) of this law for it to take effect, and the three‐month period envisaged by the first transitional provision. In these circumstances, if the Commission"s action was to be effective it also needed to be swift.

It may be worth recalling, in this context, that the Commission constantly endeavours to expedite infringement proceedings, as indeed it was called upon to do by the European Council in Amsterdam, which stressed the need to apply Community law actively in the Member States and to establish procedures that would enable problems to be resolved more quickly.

As to the protective measures:

It would be premature to say anything about a possible application to the Court of Justice for protective measures. At this stage the Commission can only reply to the question in general terms.

It is true that, because of the inherent urgency of the matter, the Commission has contemplated asking for protective measures. The reason is that action by the Commission would be fruitless if the infringement were to persist throughout the time taken to bring proceedings before the Court for failure to act.

According to Article 186 of the Treaty, the Court may order the necessary protective measures in cases brought before it. The Commission has asked the Court for protective measures in at least eleven (11) cases, within the framework of the procedure laid down in Article 169 of the Treaty. It has done so each time it felt that such measures were necessary and that the necessary conditions existed for them to be granted.

The Treaty imposes no specific limits on the power of the Court to grant protective measures. If the necessary conditions exist for it to do so, the Court generally orders the Member State to take all appropriate steps to prevent the proceedings on the merits resulting in a judgment of failure to act, which has no useful effect.

Question No 72 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0473/97)

Subject: Japanese leather industry

In response to the complaint lodged by the Confederation of National Tanners' Associations the Commission has opened an inquiry in order to determine whether or not the Japanese leather industry has derived benefit from operating a scheme which contravenes World Trade Organization rules and thus excludes all exports by the EU leather industry.

Other benefits enjoyed by the Japanese leather industry include subsidies to local manufacturers and de facto agreements between importers and dealers which in practice determine import and price levels in agreement with local producers.

In view of the importance of the leather industry in certain EU regions, could the Commission say what stage its inquiry has reached?

Answer

The Confederation of National Tanners' Association of the Community (COTANCE) lodged a complaint under (EC) Regulation No 3286/94 of 22 December 1994 (the so‐called Trade Barriers Regulation), on 24 February 1997. The Commission decided that the complaint was admissible and an investigation was formally initiated on 9 April 1997.

Community leather exports to Japan are entitled to benefit from the tariff quotas established by Japan after a 1984 GATT panel which requested it to further open its domestic market. However, COTANCE contends that the Japanese market is virtually closed to imports of finished leather from the European Community and that this is due to the following practices: a complex import licence allocation system leading to a poor exhaustion of tariff quotas, subsidization of local tanning industry and restrictive business practices of the Japanese importers of leather.

Under the Trade Barriers Regulation, the Commission conducts an investigation to verify whether the allegations of the complainant are well founded. In this context, they collect information through various interested parties and verify it. The investigation normally lasts 5 to 7 months and, once it is concluded, the Commission reports to the Advisory Committee. On this basis, a decision may be taken if an action is necessary and in the Community interest.

Thus, the Commission is now carrying out an investigation, both within the Community and Japan, into the Japanese regime of imports of leather. A report to the Member States will be presented before mid‐November.

Question No 73 by Ernesto Caccavale (H−0479/97)

Subject: European Children's Rights Day

Given the growing public awareness in Europe of the issue of child protection and with reference to the Declaration of the Rights of the Child adopted by the United Nations on 20 November 1959 and ratified on 20 November 1989 by 187 countries within the UN General Assembly in connection with the International Convention on the Rights of the Child, does the Commission intend to follow the example provided by the French law of 6 April 1996 establishing a National Children's Rights Day and actively promote the establishment of an annual European Children's Rights Day, to be held for the first time on 20 November 1997?

Answer

At the present time the Commission has no plans to propose a specific initiative for a “European Children"s Rights Day’. However, the Commission is always willing to assist the progress of activities which are primarily a matter for the Member States.

Thus, the French initiative referred to in the title of the question is one of the many activities which the Commission is supporting through appropriate budget headings.

Specifically, since September 1989, in the light of the conclusions of the Council of ministers responsible for “family’ policy, financial resources have been allocated to assist a better understanding of the deep‐seated changes brought about by demographic trends, socio‐economic problems going hand in hand with significant changes to the family structure – increased frequency of divorce, fewer marriages, declining birth rates, increasing abuse of children within the family, trafficking in children for profit, etc.

Question No 74 by Gianfranco Dell'Alba (H−0480/97)

Subject: ENEL‐ENI agreement to set up a new electricity company

On 5 May 1997 ENEL and ENI (two Italian State‐owned enterprises, in other words) signed a memorandum of understanding with a view to setting up a company that may become a leading player on the free market beginning to develop. ENI and ENEL have virtual monopolies on, respectively, the market supplying gas and fuel oils and the energy generation and distribution market, and it is highly likely that an understanding of the type in question will create further difficult barriers, preventing new competitors from entering the market just when it is about to be liberalized.

Does not the Commission believe, therefore, that the agreement could constitute a violation of Community competition rules and the legislation governing the internal market in electricity?

What steps has it taken or will it take to assess the implications of the agreement in the light of Community law before a final understanding has been concluded between ENEL and ENI?

Answer

The Commission has been informed of the signing by ENI and ENEL of a letter of intent relating to the setting‐up of a joint venture to produce electricity.

As things stand at present, and having regard to the absence of any notifiable agreement between the parties, the Commission cannot make any detailed comment on this project.

However, it is reasonable to assume that if the project goes ahead it may give rise to the creation of a concentrative joint enterprise within the meaning of Council Regulation 4064/89 of 21 December 1989. Consequently, the operation could be subject to authorization, either by the Commission or by the competent national authority, depending upon whether or not it has a Community dimension within the meaning of Regulation 4064/89.

If the Commission should be called upon to review the operation, it would of course take due account of the positions of the two parties in their respective markets and of the expected development of the electricity market before reaching its decision.

Question No 75 by Kirsten Jensen (H−0484/97)

Subject: Exports of pesticides

It has been reported in the Danish press that a Danish‐owned company is selling Paraquat to Burma. Paraquat is a toxic pesticide which is banned in Denmark and other Member States. Does the Commission intend to take the initiative and prohibit this dangerous substance in the EU?

Is it planning to prohibit sales to non‐member countries of pesticides which are prohibited on the European market?

Does it intend to take the initiative for the introduction of legislation on product liability, pursuant to which producers can be held liable for the effects on the environment and on human health of the pesticides they sell to non‐member countries?

Answer

Paraquat is an active substance used in herbicides. According to the information available to the Commission, it is currently authorised for use in 10 Member States (Belgium, Germany, Spain, France, Greece, Italy, Ireland, the United Kingdom, the Netherlands and Portugal) and not authorised in the remaining five (Austria, Denmark, Finland, Luxembourg and Sweden).

Paraquat is included in Annex I of Directive 67/548/EEC concerning classification, labelling and packaging of dangerous substances. The classification has recently been reviewed by experts and a more stringent classification has been proposed. A decision on this is scheduled to be taken later this year.

Paraquat is also currently being reviewed under Regulation 3600/92 dealing with the re‐evaluation of all the existing active substances in plant protection products covered by Directive 91/414/EEC. A decision on inclusion or exclusion of Paraquat in Annex I, the list of authorised active substances of Directive 91/414/EEC is expected in 1998.

The export of chemicals to third countries is regulated within the Community by Council Regulation 2455/92, which makes the voluntary international agreement on export/import of chemicals called the Prior Informed Consent (PIC), negotiated under the auspices of UNEP and FAO, binding for Member States. This procedure allows importing countries to make informed decisions on whether to allow or not to allow the imports of a chemical which is banned or severely restricted because of its negative effects to humans or the environment. Paraquat is not at the present time included in the PIC procedure, but it is currently being discussed for possible inclusion.

A White Paper on future Community action on environmental liability is being prepared by the Commission. A possible future Community regime in this area would be based on strict liability. A Community regime can normally not have an extra‐territorial effect, so damage caused outside the Community would not be covered by it.

Question No 76 by John Iversen (H−0485/97)

Subject: Exports of pesticides

Does the Commission intend to take the initiative for the drawing up of an international code of conduct for multinational companies which produce and sell chemical substances?

Answer

The Commission is not intending to initiate moves for an international code of conduct covering all aspects of the productions and sale of chemical products. The Commission would, however, like to draw the attention of the honourable Member that it is already extensively involved in a large number of specific actions relating to both European and international aspects of the products and sale of chemicals. To cite but two examples, the Commission has been in the forefront of international agreements to limit production and sales of ozone‐depleting substances, in particular chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs), and work with companies ensuring exports of dangerous chemicals to developing countries are made with the Prior Informed Consent. The export of chemicals to nonmember countries if regulated within the Community by Council Regulation 2455/92, which makes the voluntary international agreement on export/import of chemicals called the Prior Informed Consent (PIC) negotiated under auspices of UNEP and FAO binding for Member States. This procedure allows importing countries to make informed decisions on whether to allow or not to allow the imports of dangerous chemicals which are banned or severely restricted because of their negative effects to humans or the environment.

The Commission would also like to refer to its Communication, addressed to the European Parliament, as well as to the Council and the Economic and Social Committee, entitled ‚An industrial example’, of 30 April 1996, in which it identified more than 20 specific actions, many of which would be part of such an all‐embracing international code of conduct. In particular it cites as necessary actions, and invitation to industry to develop the Responsible Care Initiative and the favouring of an international level of environmental protection in the framework of the United Nations and the OECD.

Question No 77 by Maartje van Putten (H−0486/97)

Subject: Alternatives to tests on animals

The Commission is obliged, pursuant to Directive 93/35/EEC(3) amending for the sixth time Directive 76/768/EEC(4) on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to cosmetic products, to ban the use of experiments on animals for cosmetic purposes with effect from 1 January 1998. However, it has the option of deferral if – 'despite all reasonable endeavours' – there has been insufficient progress in developing and validating satisfactory alternatives (Article 4(1)).

Can the Commission say in which areas there has been actual progress towards the acceptance of validated methods? Can the Commission also indicate the timescale for obtaining acceptable alternatives and what efforts it will make to speed up the acceptance of alternatives in regulatory processes?

Question No 78 by Johanna Maij‐Weggen (H−0487/97)

Subject: Alternatives to tests on animals

The Commission is obliged, pursuant to Directive 93/35/EEC(5) amending for the sixth time Directive 76/768/EEC(6) on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to cosmetic products, to ban the use of experiments on animals for cosmetic purposes with effect from 1 January 1998. However, it has the option of deferral if – 'despite all reasonable endeavours' – there has been insufficient progress in developing and validating satisfactory alternatives (Article 4(1)).

In view of the very broad remit of the ECVAM, and the high priority which Parliament attaches to reducing the use of animals in experiments, can the Commission indicate how the ECVAM's activities and the fundamental and strategic research required to carry out those activities can be given more efficient back‐up? Can the Commission also indicate how current staff levels can be tailored, as soon as possible, to the extended remit of the ECVAM? Can it also indicate how the ECVAM's current level of staff relates to the budgets agreed for the ECVAM in the long‐term estimates?

Joint answer

The Commission attaches the greatest importance to the well‐being of animals. That is why it does everything possible to further the development, validation and legal acceptance of alternatives to animal experiments in the field of cosmetic products. At the present time, within the framework of Biotech II, it is allocating 3.8 MECU to three projects covering these alternative methods. Also, substantial progress has been made with percutaneous absorption and with local risks to the eyes and skin. However, these advances are not yet sufficient to result in genuine alternatives. Adopting the principle of precaution, therefore, the Commission has adopted Directive 97/18 of 17 April 1997 which defers from 1 January 1998 to 30 June 2000 the date from which it will become unlawful for ingredients and combinations of ingredients used in the composition of cosmetic products to be tested on animals. This period was felt to be sufficient for reliable alternative methods to be attained in the meantime.

With regard to ECVAM (the European Centre for the Validation of Alternative Methods), the Fourth Framework Programme for 1995‐98, granted by the CCR of Ispra, gives it a total of 13 MECU in specific appropriations to cover, among other things, laboratory needs and, in particular, research into alternative methods suitable for cosmetics. As far as human resources are concerned, the relative limitation on the size of the workforce in recent years is now gradually being made good by the new recruitment, starting this year, of at least five operatives for three years.

Question No 79 by Arthur Newens (H−0504/97)

Subject: Human rights and aid and development in the Yemen

In the Conference of Donor Countries to the Republic of Yemen, hosted by the Commission on 19‐20 June 1997, what emphasis was given to the human rights record in that country, in the light of the recent urgency resolution adopted by Parliament and of the report by Amnesty International, which is currently awaiting a response from the Yemeni authorities?

Answer

The first Consultative Group Meeting for the Republic of Yemen held in Brussels on 18‐19 June 1997, brought together representatives from 13 donor countries, 12 regional and international organizations and a large representation from the Yemeni private sector.

As in similar cases, this Consultative Group meeting was organized by the World Bank. The Commission offered logistic support including Commission premises to organize the conference. However, the Commission did neither organize nor formally host the meeting.

The Consultative Group Meeting concentrated essentially on technical issues and questions concerning details of development policy implementation. Delegations praised Yemen highly for both extremely promising progress in the implementation of its structural adjustment programme and the successful implementation of free and fair parliamentary elections on 27 April 1997 which further increased donors' confidence in the Yemeni reform process.

The Commission delegation raised the question of Human Rights' problems in Yemen referring explicitly to the European Parliament Resolution of 10 April 1997. This position was only supported by the German delegation. No other alluded to this question. In its donor's statement the Commission offered Yemen cooperation on Human Rights matters like training and awareness building. The Commission will continue to assist Yemen in institutionalising its multi‐party democratic system of government.

Donors reiterated their strong endorsement of Yemen's reform policies and pledged further considerable technical and financial assistance, which exceeded the level expected by the World Bank at the outset of the Donors Conference.

Question No 80 by Christine Oddy (H−0513/97)

Subject: Investigation into massacres in Sri Lanka

Is the Commission aware of the current inquiry into the murder of 24 Tamil civilians in the village of Kumarapuram in Trincomalee, Sri Lanka in February 1996 by military personnel?

Will the Commission support moves to ensure that the security concerns of remote villages like Kumarapuram in the North and East of Sri Lanka are given special attention and that regular visits are undertaken, under the auspices of the Human Rights Task Force, to record and take action on the concerns of civilians living there?

Answer

Sri Lanka is a country with which the EC has a long standing Cooperation Agreement and the Commission therefore has a deep interest in that country and would naturally like to see internal peace reestablished in it. The situation in Sri Lanka remains a cause for serious concern for the European Commission. The Commission is indeed aware of the murder of 24 civilians in Kumarapuam and the investigation into this unhappy affair ordered by the Government of Sri Lanka. The last session of the responsible Court was on 25 June 1997 and the next one will be held on 30 July 1997.

Unfortunately killing of civilians has been part of the conflict since it started in 1983. All ethnic communities have suffered from it and all parties to the conflict have been responsible for these acts. A few months after the above mentioned incident, 14 Sinhalese civilians were killed by the LTTE in a remote village near Wilpattu on 11 June 1996.

The only way to protect the civilians would be if all parties to the conflict would undertake to respect the principles of International Humanitarian Law and to find a negotiated settlement to the conflict.

The Sri Lankan Government established in March 1997 a Human Rights Commission with a wide mandate to monitor the human rights situation in the country.

The European Union has in its declarations and also in demarches made to the Government in Colombo urged the Government to protect the civilians and to prevent the violations of International Humanitarian Law.

Question No 81 by Caroline Jackson (H−0514/97)

Subject: EU action on imported seafood

Can the Commission explain, with regard to Commission Decision 97/334/EC(7) why the recitals refer to the testing of processed and frozen crustaceans from India, in order to deal with a potential problem found in cooked shrimp, but the articles of the decision require fresh, frozen or processed crustaceans to be tested?

Is it aware that EU action originally necessitated by the microbiological contamination of a cooked ready‐to‐eat product is now unreasonably being translated into controls on all products, including raw frozen product imported into the EU for subsequent processing?

Since this is likely to cause real problems for many small and medium‐sized companies, was the Commission's original intention to apply these rules to frozen raw products, and if so, why?

Answer

The Commission adopted December 97/334/EC(8) on the grounds of a finding of a contamination with Vibrio cholera(9) in a consignment of peeled cooked shrimps originating in India. In addition, Community inspection visits have demonstrated that there is a general problem with regard to the hygienic production and processing of shrimps in India and several Member States have discovered, during the import inspection procedures, the presence of Salmonellae in Cephalopods originating in India. It was decided, therefore, to ban imports of fresh fishery products from India and to submit consignments of frozen and processed fishery products to a microbiological test. This is the reason to make references, in the recitals of Decision 97/334, to fresh, frozen and processed products and to adopt different measures for products treated in different ways.

The Commission is now evaluating the possibility to authorize the entry into the Community of fresh, frozen products, without microbiological tests, if submitted directly after importation to a heat treatment in an EC approved establishment. If such treatment could assure that the products do not present a microbiological hazard for public health, then the Commission will propose to amend the Decision 97/334 to introduce a derogation for fresh, frozen products in the conditions described.

Question No 82 by José Escudero (H−0516/97)

Subject: Budget heading B3‐2003 'shopping list'

Could the Commission say what projects were financed in 1995 and 1996 under budget heading B3‐2003?

The budget heading B3‐2003 “Other cultural schemes implemented in the Community and in cooperation with third countries’ had an appropriation of 1.950 MECU in 1995 and 6 MECU in 1996.

‐ 50 % of the funds were allocated to heritage projects, in accordance with the rules of the “Raphael’ pilot scheme involving third countries.

The selection procedure followed the same rules as the pilot scheme itself: invitation to tender; preselection in accordance with the specifications; selection by a jury of independent experts representing all the Member States.

In 1996 these schemes represented 80 % of the appropriations. The remainder were allocated to projects relating to associate States of Central Europe, in response to proposals from Commission delegations to those countries and on the basis of the cost‐effectiveness criteria applied to the Kaleidoscope programme.

A list of the projects requested will be sent directly to the honourable Member.

Question No 83 by Michael Elliott (H−0520/97)

Subject: Animal experimentation – primates

The Commission will be aware of the publicity surrounding the statement by Professor Weissman that primates should be used to research the possible link between BSE and humans and the subsequent suggestion by two Dutch scientists that there were many chimpanzees available at the BPRC in the Netherlands for such research. In view of the enormous public protest about the moral and scientific unacceptability of carrying out BSE transmission research on chimpanzees, would the Commissioner give an assurance that not only would no EU funding be made available but also the Commission will argue strongly against such research?

Answer

In connection with Community research programmes, the Commission"s policy has been for the Fourth Framework Programme, and will be for the Fifth Framework Programme, to restrict experiments on animals to cases where they cannot be avoided. Furthermore, the Commission encourages research designed to develop alternative methods, especially through the European Centre for the Validation of Alternative Methods (ECVAM), located at Ispra in Italy. When experiments on animals cannot be avoided, they must be carried out in strict compliance with the ethical and hygienic standards in force in the country where the research takes place. The well‐being of the animals must be taken into account in the writing of the research records.

These principles apply, a fortiori, to experiments on non‐human primates. Thus, Directive 86/609/EEC stipulates that there must be strong scientific justification for such experiments. Furthermore, the Biomedical Primate Research Centre in the Netherlands bars any lethal experiments or experiments causing permanent harm to the animals. Experiments on primates in the context of BSE research, on condition that primates are suitable for research into prion diseases, would only be conceivable in the event of serious risks to public health and would be subject to rigorous methodological and ethical scrutiny.

Question No 84 by Raul Rosado Fernandes (H−0521/97)

Subject: Marketing of cereals (EPAC – Portugal)

The EPAC, a State‐owned company, which formerly held a monopoly of the market in cereals in Portugal, received a State contribution of Esc 30 bn, after obtaining a loan of Esc 50 bn to compensate for a management of the firm which brought about a deficit of the same amount, in addition to liabilities in the 1996 financial year amounting to Esc 6 bn. Competing companies asked the Commission a question on this subject. The Commission responded by instructing the Portuguese Government immediately to suspend the effects of this State guarantee in respect of all commercial activities undertaken by the EPAC in the cereals sector.

Despite this warning, the EPAC tendered on 15 May 1997, at the taxpayer's expense and on unfair terms for other companies operating in the sector, for 150 000 tonnes of maize imported from third countries. Having bid Esc 1 550 more per tonne than its rivals, it was awarded the contract.

How long is the Commission prepared to tolerate this illegal situation? What measures can it take to penalize the guilty party, namely Portugal, whose attitude is detrimental both to cereal companies and to stock farmers?

Answer

On 9 July 1997, the Commission adopted a final decision in which it ruled against the aid in the form of a guarantee granted by the Portuguese state to the EPAC. Consequently, Portugal is required to cancel the guarantee within a maximum period of two weeks.

The Commission held that this State guarantee was illegal because it was granted in breach of the Treaty rules on competition – in other words, before the Commission had been able to give its decision. In addition, the Commission found that this State guarantee failed to comply with any conditions that could be regarded as compatible with the rules of the Common Market.

The Commission also acknowledged that the measure was likely to produce a particularly destructive impact on the cereals market. For this reason, and in the absence of any action by the Portuguese authorities to eliminate the risks of distortion of competition, the Commission had previously adopted, on 30 April 1997, a decision requiring Portugal to suspend the effects of this State guarantee.

In cases such as this, involving national aid which is held to be incompatible with the Common Market, the Court of Justice has on several occasions found that the Commission may oblige the Member State to recover from the beneficiaries all the aid unlawfully granted. Consequently, and as the case involved a State guarantee whose effect was to subsidize the rate of interest on loans taken out by the EPAC, the Commission felt, in reaching the decision referred to above, that the financial advantage that had been improperly received and would have to be repaid corresponded to the difference between the Community reference rate and the rate actually paid by the EPAC on these loans.

Question No 85 by Bernie Malone (H−0522/97)

Subject: Flight‐time limitations

Can the Commission provide information about the consultations it is holding as part of its preparations for a draft directive on flight‐time limitations and indicate when it is likely to come forward with a formal proposal?

Answer

In view of the failure – over a period of five years – of the Joint Aviation Authorities to agree common rules for the regulation of flight time and rest requirements for flying crew, the Commission organized a Forum in June 1996 to assess the situation as perceived by national regulators, air operators and relevant staff. During this meeting it was apparent that there was still a wide consensus on the need to elaborate common rules in this area in order to ensure the safety of air operations and it was also clearly felt that the Commission should make further efforts to understand the points of dissent and to bridge positions in order to establish common standards, or at least a common basis, for regulating the safety aspects of flight time and rest of crew.

A task force was created for this purpose composed of experts representing the various views in this field, and it me several times between August 1996 and May 1997.

Unfortunately, despite the goodwill and the frank attitudes of all involved in this work, it has not been possible to reach a consensus on what should be a Community scheme in this domain. It was, therefore, agreed during the last meeting that the Commission should make its own assessment in the light of the material gathered and the contributions received throughout these nine months of work, and it should come forward with a proposal.

The Commission is now preparing such a proposal and intends to discuss it in the task force by the end of July. The Commission hopes to be able to formalise its position by the end of the year.

Question No 86 by James Fitzsimons (H−0524/97)

Subject: Church of Scientology

Has the Commission any powers to investigate the activities of the Church of Scientology within the European Union? Does it consider there are any mechanisms within the existing Treaty to ban such organizations from operating in the European Union or, if not, does the new Amsterdam Treaty provide the necessary mechanisms to deal with such cults and sects?

Answer

The Commission does not have any investigative powers concerning activities of sects at European level and does not collect specific information about the Church of Scientology.

The Commission is not empowered to pass judgment on the legitimacy or merits of organizations of this kind. The choice of whether or not to joint them is a matter for the individual and is guaranteed by the freedom of thought and of association.

Should it appear necessary, appropriate measures could be taken under Title VI of the Treaty on European Union to improve judicial and police cooperation on this issue.

Existing tax and labour laws might also come into play, though it is, of course, for the Member States to enforce them. Other provisions are especially relevant in that they provide for a regime to protect particular interests, such as minors.

The Amsterdam Treaty provides for more specific provisions on police and judicial cooperation, but does not refer explicitly to the phenomenon of cultus and sects.

It also specifies in a declaration to be annexed to the new Treaty, that the 'Union will respect and does not prejudice the status under national law of churches and religious associations or communities in the Member States. The Union will equally respect the status of philosophical and non‐confessional organizations.'

Question No 87 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0526/97)

Subject: Trade agreement with South Africa

Despite negotiations, no trade agreement has yet been concluded between the EU and South Africa. A trade agreement could benefit South Africa as it might result in a boost to exports and jobs, particularly in the agricultural sector. What efforts is the Commission making to give South Africa the most advantageous terms possible for its agricultural exports to the EU?

Answer

Negotiations on a bilateral Trade and Cooperation Agreement between the European Community and the Republic of South Africa began in June 1995. The proposed agreement will be of a comprehensive nature, covering all areas of mutual interest including agriculture. It will also include provisions leading to the gradual establishment of a Free Trade Area between the Parties.

In March 1996, the Community presented its detailed proposals regarding the trade chapter of the agreement. The South African side formally responded to these proposals in January 1997 and provided supplementary elements of their position during the most recent round of talks, in June 1997. Further work is required on both sides.

In the meantime, many points of principle as to the scope and nature of a Free Trade Agreement have been agreed between the Community and South Africa in terms of cooperation and improved market access. Both parties have agreed that reciprocal liberalization measures should be taken, in accordance with WTO principles and taking account of each other's sensitivities.

Since July 1996, South Africa is a full beneficiary of the revised agricultural GSP. It is the Commission's intention to ensure that the future Trade and Cooperation agreement will provide further market access opportunities for South Africa, both in the industrial and agricultural sector.

Question No 88 by Astrid Thors (H−0532/97)

Subject: Storage of radioactive waste in Estonia

There are reports of a major storage facility in Sillamäe, Estonia for waste from a factory that manufactured products containing uranium. Twelve million tonnes of radioactive waste, including 1830 tonnes of uranium, have been stored there. The storage site is one of the world's largest constructed entirely on the earth's surface. A fragile protective embankment only 10 metres wide separates the storage area from the Baltic Sea which is constantly eroding it. According to expert statements, not even the old safety requirements of the Soviet era are respected. If the site's walls cave in, the Baltic Sea will be faced with the worst disaster that has ever occurred in the area.

Is the Commission aware of the gravity of the situation and what measures is it planning to take to eliminate the risk of such a major disaster, e.g. under PHARE or its nuclear safety programme?

Answer

The European Commission is fully aware of the radioactive waste deposit at Sillamäe in Estonia. The figures quoted by the honourable Member of Parliament (1, 830 tonnes of uranium as part of 12 million tonnes of radioactive waste) are estimates, and therefore not necessarily proven to be true at this stage: the Commission's information is that whereas the 1, 830 tonnes of uranium are possible, it is a high estimate. The 12 million tonnes of radioactive waste are almost certainly a large overestimate.

The opinion of the Swedish Radiological Protection Board is that the situation is not time‐critical, although it does need to be remedied. This opinion is shared by the Estonian Government, which is considering options available for a cost‐effective and sensible solution.

The Commission is funding an on‐going multi‐country project, to review uranium mining and hot‐spot liabilities; the Estonia problem is one of these. Pilot projects are expected to demonstrate the financial viability of one‐off investment to rectify the problem now, rather than annually borrowing small tranches for ‚maintenance investment’ in order to solve the problem.

Question No 89 by Ulf Holm (H−0534/97)

Subject: EU free trade agreement with Mexico

The current free trade agreement between the EU and Mexico is shortly due to be replaced by a new agreement. Reports from Mexico say that the free trade agreements with both the EU and NAFTA have had adverse effects, inter alia in the Mexican countryside. Local production in Mexico is being wiped out by large‐scale, intensive agricultural production which is often run with numerous subsidies, using artificial fertilizer and chemical pesticides.

In the light of the above, will the Commission say whether it has carried out any analysis of the social and environmental impact, for example, of the present free trade agreement between the EU and Mexico?

Does the Commission intend to replace the present free trade agreement with an even more comprehensive agreement? If so, in what sectors?

Answer

In July 1996 the Commission evaluated the conformity of a possible Free Trade Agreement with Mexico – which does not exist yet – with WTO rules, as well as studying the effect of a possible FTA on the Community common policies, and its other partners. These studies were those required by the Council's Conclusions of 22 June 1995. The Commission will shortly prepare the further studies required by the Council's Conclusions of 29 April 1997. These will address the extent to which the agreement will achieve Community economic interest, the political and other benefits of the agreement, whether it would support the development of multilateral trading systems, its overall economic effect and its impact on the Community's other external commitments.

Question No 90 by Jan Bertens (H−0579/97)

Subject: EU negotiations with Mexico on a trade and cooperation agreement

Does the Commission agree that it would have been better if it had negotiated with the Mexican Government after the elections rather than just before? If not, why not? In my opinion, it would have been better if Mexico had first been able to show through elections that it was ready for the agreement. There were, moreover, too many irregularities in the run‐up to the elections.

Why did the Commission take it upon itself to depart from the standard wording of the democracy and human rights clause? Doing so, after all, gives the wrong signal, especially in view of the Mexican Government's reluctance to accept a democracy and human rights clause.

Answer

The Commission regrets that it is unable to share the honourable Member"s opinion regarding the timing of the negotiations with Mexico.

1. First, the following points should be borne in mind:

‐ that the two parties, in the Solemn Declaration of Paris on 2 May 1995, had confirmed their desire to conclude an agreement at an early date,

‐ that the negotiating brief had been approved by the Council in June 1996,

‐ that the first session of negotiations had taken place in October 1996,

‐ and that the Council had, in April 1996, approved supplementary guidelines allowing negotiations to be resumed.

Under these conditions, deferring the negotiations until after the election date would have been difficult to defend politically.

Secondly, it transpired that the Mexican Government, for reasons of internal politics, preferred to conclude the negotiations before the elections, and that it therefore demonstrated greater flexibility than might have been assumed, particularly in accepting, as regards human rights, enacting terms (Article 1, “fundamental aspects’ clause – Article 58, “non‐performance article’ – Article 39, cooperation on human rights) which go very much further than in the Community"s other agreements.

2. It is not correct to say that the Commission departed in the negotiations from the standard clause on democracy and human rights. In this context, it should be recalled that:

‐ the text provisionally accepted by the Commission for Article 1, reading “... shall inspire the policies of the parties...’ rather than “... internal and external policies ...’ does not, according to the confirmed opinion of the legal services of the Commission and the Council, constitute a departure from the negotiating brief,

‐ while defending this argument, the Commission preferred not to initial this text, in order to have the opportunity to consult all the Member States, especially with regard to this Article 1,

‐ when it became apparent that the Member States insisted that the text of Article 1 should remain unchanged, Mexico, in evaluating the overall result of the negotiations, declared its willingness to reconsider its position on this point.

It is thus highly probable that negotiations will very shortly end in a result that is entirely satisfactory for the Community as far as the advancement of human rights and democracy in Mexico is concerned.

Question No 91 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0535/97)

Subject: Follow‐up to the Lindholm report on equal treatment and non‐discrimination with regard to the European Communities' staff regulations and conditions of employment

In its opinion (904(CNS)) on Parliament's amendments to COM(96)77, the Commission notes that, whilst the substance of the amendments is not being called into question, it is not an appropriate time, after four years' of legislative procedure, to incorporate them. Given that a new Article 6a was added to the EC Treaty and existing Articles 2 and 3 were supplemented at Amsterdam, authorizing the taking of appropriate action to combat discrimination based for instance on sex and sexual orientation and requiring the Communities to promote equality between men and women, will the Commission state the grounds on which it considers this not to be an appropriate time to incorporate the amendments Parliament adopted by a large majority, when those amendments were intended to help fulfil the objectives of the Treaty and the undertakings entered into as regards employees of the EU?

Answer

First, the Commission must make it clear that it has always systematically opposed any form of discrimination, including discrimination based on sexual orientation, and that the amendment to the staff regulations proposed by the Commission envisages, inter alia, an explicit provision in Article 1a to the effect that officials shall be entitled, under the application of the regulations, to equality of treatment without direct or indirect reference to, for example, their sexual orientation.

This amendment is fully consistent with the decisions of the Amsterdam Council, which proposed amending the Treaty establishing the European Community by inserting a new Article 6A relating to non‐discrimination and amending Articles 2 and 3 on equality between men and women.

With regard to the amendments to the Lindholm report adopted by the European Parliament, those amendments referred to, among other things, recognition in the staff regulations of the situation of unmarried couples, whether homosexual or heterosexual, whereas at present the provisions of the regulations govern only the family status founded on marriage.

These amendments went far beyond the intentions and objectives stated in those conclusions of the Amsterdam Council to which the honourable Member referred in her oral question.

The Commission has recently adopted measures favourable to unmarried couples, both officials and other staff. Those measures involve an initial recognition of cohabitation situations and the granting to those concerned of certain administrative, non‐statutory and financially neutral facilities.

Question No 92 by Marianne Eriksson (H−0539/97)

Subject: Homosexuals in the EU institutions

One of the Commission's arguments for not incorporating Parliament's amendment concerning equal rights of partners irrespective of sex and sexual orientation in its proposal to amend the Communities' staff regulations and conditions of employment – despite the express objective of the proposal being to combat discrimination between staff – has been the financial burden on the Communities that would result from implementing Parliament's amendment.

Has the Commission assessed the financial implications? If so, is the cost so high that the Commission considers that the principle of equal treatment should not apply to all EU staff? Has the Council been notified of the assessment and the arguments it has produced? Has the Council received details of it?

Answer

In general terms, the Commission considers that no budgetary justification can be cited in support of maintaining discriminatory treatment that is contrary to the general principles of law.

The Commission must make it clear that it has always systematically opposed any form of discrimination, including discrimination based on sexual orientation, and that the amendment to the staff regulations for officials and other staff of the Community proposed by the Commission and currently being studied by the Council envisages, inter alia, an explicit provision in Article 1a to the effect that officials shall be entitled, under the application of the regulations, to equality of treatment without direct or indirect reference to, for example, their sexual orientation.

At present, the staff regulations for Community officials and other staff only take married couples into account for the purposes of granting certain forms of compensation and allowances. Extending the availability of these benefits to unmarried couples, whether homosexual or heterosexual, as envisaged in certain amendments to the Lindholm report adopted by the European Parliament, was a measure designed not to eliminate a de facto form of discrimination but to treat in the same way two situations that are legally different. One of those situations is cohabitation and the other is marriage, and marriage alone is at present taken into account in the staff regulations.

On the basis of certain projections undertaken by the Commission, initial estimates showed that granting to homosexual or heterosexual couples certain benefits currently reserved for married couples would result in a quite substantial increase in the burden on the institutions" budgets. In view of the impossibility of obtaining sufficiently accurate figures in this area, the results of these estimates have not been released. However, it is essentially for the legal reasons mentioned earlier that the Commission has maintained its proposal as it stands, which does not exclude the possibility that the amendments proposed by the European Parliament may be taken into account in due course, when the amended version of the staff regulations is considered in broader terms.

Question No 93 by Heidi Hautala (H−0544/97)

Subject: Criteria for determining sites to be included in the Natura 2000 network

Opinions have been drawn up in Finland concerning the country's proposal for the areas to be included in the Natura 2000 environmental protection programme. Various authorities, including the Ministry of Transport, are proposing that the entire Natura programme should be the subject of an environmental impact assessment under the terms of the corresponding law and that the impact of the programme on the long‐term planning of the transport network should be determined.

Does the Commission regard it as appropriate that a state authority should be recommending criteria for determining sites to be included in the Natura 2000 network which are at odds with the case law of the Court of Justice of the European Communities as reflected in its judgment in Case C 44/95 (the so‐called Lappel Bank case)?

Is the Commission aware that in their opinions the local authorities, including the City of Helsinki, are already expressing reservations at this stage with regard to road and port construction projects, disregarding the fact that, as a result, the third most important water bird habitat in the Gulf of Finland would be excluded from the proposal, although there is a very strong case for its inclusion on ornithological grounds?

Can the Commission confirm the criteria for the establishment of the Natura 2000 network?

Answer

The first question relates to the criteria to be used when selecting sites for Natura 2000 network. It is understood that the Finnish conservation authorities accept that only biological criteria in accordance with the provisions of Annex III (Stage 1) of the Habitat Directive and the Santona and Lappel Bank judgments should be used in site selection and when setting site boundaries. It is for the Ministry of the Environment to see that when the national list of sites is now further developed for government decision on the basis of comments received, inappropriate arguments do not influence this process.

The second part of the question refers to comments of the City of Helsinki pertaining to one particular site, Porvarinlahti Bay, which is proposed to be on the national list.

There is a complaint concerning this site (P−97/4249) mainly because its boundaries are claimed to exclude an area in order to facilitate a proposed major harbour development. The ornithological value on the basis of the Birds Directive of this site is well established and it has been proposed in the draft national list. The City of Helsinki will be well aware of the procedure according to Birds and Habitats Directives in this matter. It is again for the Finnish Ministry of the Environment to see that this matter will be dealt with in accordance with the provisions of Annex III (Stage 1) of the Habitats Directive and the Santona and Lappel Bank judgments. Taking account of socio‐economic matters is again possible afterwards for proposed sites following the procedure laid down in Article 6(3) and (4) of the Habitats Directive.

The third part of the question asks whether the Commission can confirm the criteria for the establishment of the Natura 2000 network.

The Commission can indeed confirm that the criteria to be used are the specific scientific criteria provided in Annex III of the Habitats Directive.

Question No 94 by Raymonde Dury (H−0546/97)

Subject: Disproportion in the allocation of European grants to Belgium minimum wage earners

The TOR‐EFD (Emploi et Fonction des Défavorisés) association was given responsibility by the Belgian Federal Government for allocating European Social Fund grants to finance job placement projects for persons dependent on the minimex (minimum income) paid by public social aid commissions (CPAS) in Belgian municipalities. A representative of the European Commission is a member of the monitoring committee for the federal programme in respect of Objective 3 and the European Social Fund, chaired by the Minister for Employment and Labour.

Would the Commission state whether its representative mentioned in his reports the clear imbalance between the proportion of disadvantaged persons in the various regions of Belgium and the number of projects approved over the past ten years for each of these regions? If so, what measures has he put forward to offset this obvious imbalance and what decisions will the Commission take?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the concerns raised by the honourable Member regarding possible unequal distribution of the finances managed by ‚TOK‐EFD’, the NGO promoter managing the Objective 3 Operational Programme of the European Social Fund, under the framework established by the federal Ministry of Labour and Employment.

In considering this matter two particular points need to be borne in mind. First, Hainaut is an Objective 1 region and is not eligible for funding through the federal programme for Objective 3. This has an impact on the geographical distribution figures for Objective 3. Second, no requests for funding from the Public Centres for Social Aid (CPAS) of Wallonia were refused by TOK‐EFD prior to November 1996.

At both meetings the Commission representative expressed concern at the composition of the Administrative Council insofar as this might prejudice a balanced selection of projects. In the framework of the mid‐term review of the Operational Programme, the Monitoring Committee has requested that additional resources be made available as a matter of priority to redress the geographical imbalance and avoid interrupting the good work being carried out for marginalized persons by the Flemish CPAS.

The Commission will continue to monitor this problem.

Question No 95 by Bernd Posselt (H−0548/97)

Subject: Promotion of culture in Euro‐regions

What opportunities currently exist for cultural cooperation in the Euro‐regions between the EU and the countries of Central and Eastern Europe, and where is the Commission placing the emphasis for the future in this sphere, not least where the cultivation of minorities languages is concerned?

Answer

The PHARE programme for the countries of Central and Eastern Europe has made it possible to finance cultural cooperation activities through programmes such as the Cross‐Border and Partnership programmes and a few national programmes, for example in Bulgaria and Romania.

The PHARE programme was organized on a demand‐driven basis. That means that the projects financed are, in the main, those submitted by the countries in question. It must be acknowledged that, with a few exceptions, cultural cooperation has not yet been identified as a priority.

With more specific reference to the protection of linguistic minorities, the Democracy programme allows the financing of schemes to support ethnic minorities. Such projects often incorporate action to defend the cultural and linguistic heritage.

In the context of the pre‐accession strategy, the Community cultural and audiovisual programmes will be opened up for participation by the candidate States on the basis of specific arrangements laid down in the additional protocols to the European agreements, thus allowing closer cooperation between them and the Member States, based on the acquis communautaire and the Community"s priorities in the cultural and audiovisual fields.

Question No 96 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0549/97)

Subject: Operational programme for combating exclusion from the labour market

The operational programme for combating exclusion from the labour market submitted by Greece for the period 1994‐1999 and approved by the Commission is a valuable multi‐dimensional approach consisting of a large number of complementary measures designed to meet the challenges of poverty and social exclusion. In view of the high levels of poverty in Greece as a whole, and as the social categories frequently excluded from the support and protection networks are faced with the most acute forms of marginalization, will the Commission state why there is such a low take up rate for the programme and whether there are any thoughts of amending it with a view to strengthening or improving the existing structures?

Answer

As already mentioned in the Commission answer to the honourable Member's written question 586/96, the low credit absorption so far of the Operational Programme ‚Combating Exclusion from the Labour Market’ is mainly due to the difficulties in applying the Operational Programme's innovative approach as well as difficulties during the 199596 period in particular to establish and apply systems and procedures that would guarantee more effective implementation.

The need to strengthen and improve existing structures, and, if necessary, create new structures was emphasized by the Commission even before the approval of the Operational Programme in question and was explicitly stated in the 1994‐99 Community Support Framework for Greece.

In the discussion which has already started between the Commission and the Greek authorities in the framework of the midterm review and the subsequent modification of the Human Resources Operational Programmes, the question of the definition of appropriate policies to better address the problems of excluded persons as well as the urgent need to identify and strengthen or develop the necessary structures to support such policies is the main priority.

Question No 98 by Tommy Waidelich (H−0557/97)

Subject: Disruption of the internal market

For some time there have been serious problems with ships flying flags of convenience within the internal market. Certain countries have attempted to build up fleets at the expense of other countries by granting tax concessions. This problem has intensified recently since the Netherlands and Norway slashed taxes in the shipping industry. I have also been informed that there is the same risk of 'flags of convenience' in the road haulage business to the advantage of countries with lighter taxation. Such measures disrupt the pattern of transport within the Union and have a negative impact on employment in some Member States.

Does the Commission consider that preferential treatment of certain sectors through individual countries offering tax concessions distorts competition in the internal market and does it intend to take the necessary measures to remedy these problems?

Answer

The flagging out of ships formerly registered in EC Member States to so‐called flags of convenience has had a negative effect on the development of the Community's maritime industry for many years. Studies have identified differences in fiscal treatment between flags of convenience on the one hand and EC registers as being the main distortive factor.

Member States have responded to this situation by drawing up support schemes for their national fleets.

In order to promote a safe and competitive Community fleet with the employment of the highest possible number of Community seafarers, and to increase the effectiveness of the national schemes, the Commission has recently issued revised State aid guidelines for aid to shipping. These guidelines allow Member States to introduce fiscal relief measures for their national shipping sectors. The conditions for such aid and the reasons behind the Commission's policy are explained in more detail in the revised guidelines.

The Commission is not aware of the existence of a comparable situation in the road haulage sector.

Question No 99 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0559/97)

Subject: Refusal of the Turkish‐Cypriot regime to allow Eleni Foka to return to the occupied part of Cyprus

On 27.5.1997 Eleni Foka, a Greek‐Cypriot teacher from Karpasia, went to the free area of Cyprus in order to receive medical treatment. Since 13.6.1997 when she wished to return to her home, her school and her pupils in the occupied territories, she had been denied access, because she refuses to sign a declaration that she 'recognises' the Turkish‐Cypriot 'state' of northern Cyprus.

Will the Commission say whether it is aware of this incident and what action it intends to take to enable this Greek‐Cypriot teacher to return home?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the situation which the honourable Member has described.

According to the information received by the Commission, the United Nations, which has jurisdiction in matters associated with the enclaves in the northern part of Cyprus, is currently endeavouring to find a solution to this problem.

The facts recounted by the honourable Member show the importance of reaching a political settlement to the Cyprus question. Face‐to‐face talks between Mr Clerides and Mr Denktash began, under the aegis of the UN, on 9 July, and the Commission hopes that these discussions will allow progress to be made towards a political solution.

As the Commission said previously, in its answer to Mr Kaklamanis"s written question no 2294/97, a successful outcome to the discussions between Mr Clerides and Mr Denktash will facilitate the negotiations concerning Cyprus"s accession to the Community, which are to begin shortly.

The prospect of Cyprus"s accession to the Union offers, in fact, a unique opportunity to find a solution to the Cyprus problem. Accession will benefit both communities in the island; among other things, it will enable problems such as that raised by the honourable Member to be solved in accordance with European principles and legislation.

Question No 100 by Hugh Kerr (H−0562/97)

Subject: EU‐Australia Cooperation Agreement

Why has the Commission allowed the Australian Government off the hook regarding human rights by agreeing to sign the Political Declaration with Australia, rather than the Framework Agreement which was first envisaged?

Is the Commission not concerned that this may set a precedent for countries who want to avoid a commitment to human rights when negotiating cooperation agreements with the European Union?

Answer

The European Community and its Member States adopted a Joint Declaration with Australia which was signed in Luxembourg on 26 June 1997. This Joint Declaration reflects the common resolve to cooperate across the many areas in which Australia and the EU have shared interests, to strengthen relations and to endow them with a longterm perspective. The Declaration underlines the common goal of building upon partnership in order to support democracy, the rule of law and respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms. The European Union and Australia will consult bilaterally and within the framework of the relevant bodies of the UN on human rights issues in general and, in particular, on how to advance their shared objectives of promoting human rights internationally.

During negotiations aimed at the conclusion with Australia of a Framework Agreement on Trade and Cooperation, this common commitment to the respect and enhancement of human rights was never put into doubt. Australia questioned, however, the inclusion of provisions enabling a party to take appropriate measures, which may consist in the termination or suspension of the agreement, in case of a human rights infringement. Following the Council decision of 29 May 1995, these provisions are included in all Community agreements with third countries. The difference of views on this matter was one of the main reasons that made it impossible to agree on a Framework Agreement with Australia. Since the Joint Declaration is not legally binding on either side, the above‐mentioned provisions do not apply to it.

The EU was not prepared to agree to the exclusion of a binding human rights clause from a Framework Agreement. Consequently no precedent has been set for countries which may want to avoid binding commitments on human rights. It is therefore not appropriate to speak of letting the Australian Government off the hook.

***

Question No 101 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0564/97)

Subject: Protection of cultural sites and monuments from commercial exploitation

The Greek Tourist Board (EOT) has already begun to cede ancient theatres to private individuals for commercial exploitation, and limited companies are being set up to manage all its property assets.

Will the Commission say whether the cession of sites and monuments of world‐wide historical and archaeological importance to private companies with the aim of generating profit is at odds with resolutions and decisions taken by the European Parliament and the EU in favour of the preservation of the cultural heritage, and outline the measures it intends to take to prevent their exploitation to the detriment of the interests of society as a whole?

Answer

As the honourable Member is aware, within the framework of the principle of subsidiarity and according to Article 128 of the EC Treaty, the protection of the national cultural heritage is the exclusive responsibility of the national competent authorities.

The role of the Community and the Commission in particular is to encourage cooperation between Member States through programmes which aim at preserving and enhancing the common European cultural heritage while at the same time they respect the national and regional cultural diversities.

In this context, the leasing of archaeological sites and of the ancient theatres to private concerns by the Greek authorities does not in any way contravene any Community regulation.

The Commission is certain that the Greek competent authorities will ensure that stringent rules are put in place so that any private lease holder will be obliged to undertake measures for the protection, maintenance and enhancement of the archaeological sites in question as well as to maintain the general public's easy access.

Question No 102 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0565/97)

Subject: Need for measures to prevent a monopoly in the international aviation industry by the merger of Boeing and McDonnell Douglas

The decision taken by the American administration unconditionally to approve the merger of Boeing and McDonnell Douglas which establishes a world‐wide monopoly will have adverse effects for the aviation industry and employment, but also for smaller airlines which will have to face not only a trade monopoly but also the problem of exclusive cooperation contracts such as Boeing has already introduced. It will also have particularly adverse consequences for the defence industry of European countries.

Will the Commission say what measures it intends to take to protect the European aviation and defence industry, European airlines and, above all, consumers who will be required to bear the increased cost caused by a monopoly in this market?

Answer

As the Honourable MEP knows, the concentration has been notified to the Commission on 18 February 1997. The Commission initiated proceedings under the Merger Control Regulation, in order to see whether the notified concentration would create or strengthen a dominant position. A Statement of Objections has been sent out on 21 May 1997. A final deadline for a Commission decision is 31 July 1997.

With regard to the notified operation, the Commission has identified three areas of competition concerns, that is:

‐ the combination of Boeing's and McDonnell Douglas' civil aircraft businesses, which is expected to considerably strengthen Boeing's dominant position in the market for large commercial aircraft,

‐ the already existing and possible further exclusive agreements with major airlines and their unprecedented duration,

‐ the spillover effects stemming from the large increase in Boeing's defence and space business, in terms of overall financial resources, greater access to technology developed by publicly funded R&D, increased bargaining power vis‐à‐vis equipment suppliers and off‐set deals.

On the contrary of the civil side, the investigation did not reveal dominance concerns in the defence and military sector.

Under the Merger procedure, the Parties may suggest modifications to the initial concentration plan, with a view to supplying appropriate remedial solutions. The Commission is currently exploring whether Boeing would offer remedies likely to remove the competition problems identified.

If adequate remedies are not agreed on in respect to these competition problems, the Commission would then have no other choice than to prohibit the concentration as incompatible with the common market. Adequate remedies could eventually trigger a conditional clearance by the Commission.

An antitrust investigation has also been carried out in the USA by the Federal Trade Commission. Throughout the proceedings, in compliance with the bilateral cooperation agreement on competition policy, the European Commission and the FTC have carried out all necessary notifications. They have in particular informed each other on all important steps of the procedure.

The fact that on 1 July 1997, the FTC has reached a majority decision not to oppose the merger does not alter the serious concerns expressed by the European Commission. As the criteria laid down in the European and the US merger control systems do not match in all their aspects, it can never be excluded that two competition authorities reach a different conclusion in an individual case. In such a situation, the merging companies would have to respect the negative decision by the one authority, since the positive decision by the other does not mean that the parties must carry out the merger. In practice, however, the companies concerned try to satisfy both competition authorities.
