Question No 103 by Mary Banotti (H−0566/97)

Subject: Discrimination on travelling with Eurostar

Is the Commission aware that Eurostar requires its passengers to be on the UK electoral roll to qualify for the Rewards Programme?

For example an EU citizen living and working in the UK and travelling frequently to mainland Europe via the Eurostar is discriminated against.

Can the Commission investigate whether this proviso infringes Community regulations?

Answer

The Commission is aware that the Eurostar Frequent Traveller Programme, which was introduced in autumn 1996, was initially launched in the UK, France and Belgium, because the company considered these countries as its main market.

The Programme is not restricted to those holding British, French or Belgian citizenship or who appear on the relevant electoral roll. The only provision is a mailing address in one of these Member States.

The Commission has been informed that, in the near future, there shall be an extension of the Eurostar Frequent Traveller Programme to other than the above mentioned Member States due to the development of the market.

Question No 104 by Marie‐Paule Kestelijn‐Sierens (H−0569/97)

Subject: Results of research into the effectiveness of homeopathy and other therapeutic methods

Can the Commission indicate how the funding available since 1994 for research into homeopathic and other therapeutic methods has been utilized, what precise research has been carried out and whether such research has already yielded any results? If so, what conclusions can be drawn? When will such results be forwarded to the European Parliament? Otherwise, when can they be expected? Does the Commission intend to continue its research? Is it providing any additional funding for this?

Answer

Under the aegis of the Commission, an advisory group known as the Homeopathic Medicine Research Group was set up, comprising homeopaths, experts in clinical and pharmacological research and methodologists (statisticians and epidemiologists). A budget of ECU 1 million (budget heading APAS B6‐8332) has been allocated to this group. It was given one year in which fulfil its mandate, which was to consider the possibility of undertaking research into homeopathy and the possible methods and standards.

In August 1996 the group submitted a report including recommendations on the application of specific protocols for homeopathic research and the continuous improvement of such protocols with a view to improving the quality and recognition of clinical research into homeopathy. That report is available to Parliament, and a copy will be sent directly to the honourable Member.

Despite Parliament"s express desire to allocate a budget to research into the efficacy of alternative forms of medicine, it was not legally possible to set up any such research activities within the framework of the BIOMED programme (budget heading B6‐7412) because alternative forms of medicine are not included among the objectives of the specific programme as adopted by the Council after consulting Parliament.

With regard to the 5th Framework Programme, in the proposal for a European Parliament and Council Decision presented by the Commission (COM(97) 642 Final) homeopathy is not at present included among the programme"s priorities.

Question No 105 by Leen van der Waal (H−0570/97)

Subject: Canning of intervention beef for food aid

There has been an enormous increase in the European Union's intervention stocks of beef, not least because of the BSE crisis. At the same time there is a demand for canned beef products for food aid purposes. For example, North Korea recently submitted an official request to the Commission for 10 000 tonnes of canned beef.

Why has the Commission still not decided to can some of the beef held in intervention so that it can be disposed of as food aid?

Answer

Although purchases of beef into intervention in recent weeks have somewhat slowed down it is beyond dispute that the Community intervention stocks have been rapidly building up since Spring of 1996. It is obviously in the interest of all concerned that the disposal of those stocks will be performed in an orderly manner once the market situation permits so.

Turning part of the intervention beef into processed cannot products for food‐aid purposes may at first sight appear to be an appropriate measure. However, food‐aid channelled through either bilateral contacts or international organizations is typically very specific with regard to the composition and packing of the products concerned taking into account the distribution to the final recipients. Rather than producing and storing certain cannot products for which there may never be a formal request for food‐aid delivery, as internationally defined, the Commission would rather prefer to respond to requests on an ad hoc basis.

With regard to the specific situation in North Korea, the Commission has been following very closely humanitarian developments for some considerable time. On 23 May 1997, the Commission announced a forthcoming decision for approximately 155 000 tonnes of food products to be supplied as food aid for vulnerable population groups in North Korea. This food aid (valued at 46.3 MECU) will be supplied mostly in the form of cereals, about 135 000 tonnes, and supplemented with complementary foods. Delivery of the food aid will be carried out in close cooperation with the UN's World Food Programme. Additional support (9.5 MECU) is also being provided in the field of public health and nutritional assistance. Both these actions are in addition to previously decided humanitarian assistance in food aid and nutritional support amounting to nearly 10 MECU granted through NGOs and international agencies to respond to needs following serious flooding that occurred in 1995 and 1996.

Question No 106 by Bárbara Dührkop Dührkop (H−0572/97)

Subject: The position of President Santer and the Commission on quota hopping

There have been many articles in the press recently about the letter from the Commission President to the British Prime Minister on the problem of quota hopping. There have been comments to the effect that, judging from Mr Santer's initiative and the substance of his letter, the Commission President's position contradicts that of his fellow‐Commissioners and the case‐law of the Court of Justice.

Can the Commission confirm, as the Court of Justice has done, that the requirement that the crews of British‐registered vessels must be resident in the United Kingdom is contrary to Community legislation?

Can the Commission confirm, as the Court of Justice did in the Jaderow judgment, that Community legislation authorizes vessels to unload their catches in any Community port, and that no one can insist that all or part of such catches be unloaded in a given country?

Answer

The Commission fully endorses the case‐law of the Court of Justice regarding the exercise of the freedom of establishment in the fisheries sector.

According to the Court, Community law as it now stands, does not preclude a Member State, in authorizing one of its vessels to fish against national quotas, from laying down conditions designed to ensure that the vessel has a real economic link with that State if that link concerns only the relations between that vessel's fishing operations and the populations dependent on fisheries and related industries.

The Commission is of the opinion that landing obligations or obligations related to the residence of the crew cannot be imposed on fishing operators. However, fishing operators can prove the existence of an economic link with the flag State either by landing a proportion of catches in the ports of the flag State, or by operating with a proportion of crew normally resident ashore in the flag State or by other relevant means of their choice.

In any case, any national requirements designed to ensure the existence of a real economic link between the fishing vessel and the flag State must be non‐discriminatory, alternative and proportionate to their aim.

***

Question No 107 by Lone Dybkjær (H−0574/97)

Subject: Marketing of breast‐milk substitutes

In 1981, the WHO adopted a code designed to protect women against the marketing of breast‐milk substitutes persuading them not to breastfeed. According to the WHO, inappropriate use of breast‐milk substitutes causes the death of 1.5 million babies every year in the third world alone. The EU endorses the code and the WHO's subsequent resolutions in Council resolution 92/C 172/01. Article 2 of that resolution states that EU delegations in third countries should serve as contact points for complaints about marketing in those countries by producers from the Member States. Under Article 5, the Commission is to forward a report to Parliament and the Council every two years on the application of the resolution.

Some companies receiving aid for exports to third countries of breast‐milk substitutes are apparently not complying with the code.

Does the Commission believe that companies in breach of these rules should continue to receive export subsidies?

Has the Commission complied with the provision in Article 5 requiring it to forward a report on compliance with resolution 92/C 172/01?

What practical measures does the Commission plan to take to help the authorities in developing countries ensure that companies from the Member States do not breach the relevant rules?

Answer

The Council resolution of 18 June 1992 on the marketing of breast‐milk substitutes in third countries by Community based manufacturers recognized that the application of the international code on the marketing of breast‐milk substitutes would ensure that marketing practices should not discourage mothers from breastfeeding.

It recognized also that the Community cannot legislate for third countries and therefore that it was for those countries to take the necessary measures to apply the international code in their territory as appropriate.

In respect of this principle the Community designated the Commission and its delegations to serve as contact points and receive any complaints or criticisms from the authorities of third countries with respect to the marketing practices of Community based manufacturers.

As replied to relevant written questions the Commission and its delegations have received no such complaint or criticism from the authorities of a third country which would justify the drawing up of a report to the Council and the European Parliament on the matter or which would induce any action in conformity with the Council resolution.

Export refunds serve two main economic purposes. Firstly they compensate for the different between the prices of agricultural raw materials on the world and Community markets which enables Community exporters to be competitive on third country markets. Secondly they help to stabilise agricultural markets within the Community by facilitating the export of Community agricultural products including when exported in the form of certain goods. As such they have both industrial and agricultural policy objectives.

It is for this reason that processed agricultural goods are eligible for a refund in respect of incorporated agricultural products. Breast milk substitutes are eligible goods and as such are submitted to the same treatment and conditions as other processed agricultural goods.

There is no apparent reason why breast milk substitutes should be subjected to additional requirements since it is primarily the responsibility of the country where the goods are marketed to determine acceptable marketing/promotional practices and for ensuring that these are enforced and respected. In addition any measures taken at the Community level would not achieve the objective sought.

***

Question No 108 by Liam Hyland (H−0582/97)

Subject: Aiding Ireland's alternative energy potential

In the context of the Commission's proposals for a new five‐year programme for promoting renewable energy sources (1998‐2002), in what way is the Altener II programme likely to make a worthwhile contribution to alternative energy policies in Ireland, taking into account the potential of Ireland's natural resources, wind, wave, tide, turf (peat), waste, crop and short‐rotation forestry?

Answer

The Commission co‐funded, in the framework of the ALTENER I programme, a project in the planning infrastructures sub‐sector entitled 'Total Renewable Energy Resource in Ireland' carried out by ESB International and ETSU.

The final report is being analyzed at the moment. The project aimed at evaluating the Practicable Resources to generate electricity from RES take into account the expected demand at short, medium and long term (2005, 2010, 2020).

This project is not a general plan – heating and electricity production not connected to the grid are not included – nevertheless, the Commission would consider, after analysing it, the possibility of co‐funding an implementation plan in the future.

ALTENER II, which has not yet been approved by the Council, will continue to support planning infrastructures (action under art.2b) and will support targeted actions (under art. 2e) intended to increase the RES installed capacities and promote private and public investments by providing financial support in order to reduce the peripheral and service cost of RES large scale prospects.

***

Question No 109 by Gerard Collins (H−0584/97)

Subject: Decrease in Irish projects under the EU's LIFE programme for environmental projects

Can the Commission explain why in 1996 only one project from Ireland secured funding from the EU's LIFE programme for environmental projects and why in 1997 only two Irish projects are to be co‐financed from this programme?

Answer

During the year 1996 Ireland submitted 9 projects or 1.5 % of the total of 601 projects submitted to LIFE by the Member States. Of those 9, 3 were preselected in consultation with the Irish authorities to be submitted to the independent expert evaluation groups.

The results of this evaluation were favourable to only one of the 3 projects considered, which therefore received co‐financing from LIFE.

This was a project from AHP Manufacturing b.v. relating to the development of an integrated monitoring system for atmospheric emissions.

In 1997 the situation improved, thanks to the efforts made by the Irish authorities to provide information. Ireland has submitted 18 projects this year, of which 7 were preselected for expert evaluation.

It should be added that the selection undertaken by the Commission, the national authorities and the independent experts is based not on national quotas but on the quality of the project, meaning the purely technical and/or ecological advances that may be found in the demonstration projects.

The Commission"s decision on the projects to be financed in 1997 will be reached before the end of July 1997.

***

Question No 110 by Brian Crowley (H−0585/97)

Subject: New EU actions for the elderly and the socially excluded

Following the Amsterdam Summit, what course of action does the Commission propose to take to ensure that in the future properly funded actions will be made possible for both elderly persons, NGOs working on their behalf and the socially excluded, with particular regard to the need for a new and comprehensive programme to combat poverty?

Answer

The Commission is in the process of analysing the scope for action which the new Treaty will offer, once it is ratified, in the field of social exclusion, poverty and the elderly. This analysis is not yet complete.

A first analysis has shown that Article 6A, and Articles 117 and 118(2), and the new Chapter on Employment in the new Treaty could offer scope for strengthening Community action in these fields.

. Article 6A will allow the Commission in consultation with the European Parliament to propose appropriate action to combat discrimination including that based on age. Proposals will require the approval of all Member States.

. In Article 117 of Chapter 4 (Social Policy), 'the combating of social exclusion' is listed as one of the 6 objectives of social policy. The specific references tot he European Social Charter of 1961 and the 1989 Community Charter of the Fundamental Social Rights of Workers may broaden the scope for action.

. A new paragraph in Article 118(2) of the new Treaty allows for qualified majority decisions by the Council of 'measures designed to encourage cooperation between Member States through initiatives aimed at improving knowledge, developing exchanges of information and best practices, promoting innovative approaches and evaluating experiences in order to combat social exclusion'. The legal basis created by this article could make it possible to propose new measures to combat social exclusion.

. The new Employment Chapter establishes the basis for defining and implementing coordination of the Member States' employment policies. A more effective employment strategy is crucial in terms of tackling poverty and social exclusion.

***

Question No 111 by Pat Gallagher (H−0587/97)

Subject: Conditions in Spital Neuropsihici Institution in Siret, Romania

The European Union, through the Phare programme, has made resources available to the Spital Neuropsihici Institution in Siret. However, the conditions under which the children are kept and the treatment they receive are still well below the norm in a European Union orphanage/hospital.

Has the Commission undertaken any inspection visits to this institution and, if not, will it now do so as a matter of urgency with a view to satisfying itself that the children in this institution benefit from the highest possible standards of care and treatment?

Answer

In 1993‐94 and 1994‐6, through the “Energy Programme’, PHARE financed the installation of electric heaters and supplies of fuel oil for the institution at Siret, which is under the responsibility of the Ministry of Health. However, these activities were not such as to bring about any radical improvement in the way the children are kept or the treatment they receive, and their living conditions give cause for concern.

The reasons for this are as follows:

‐ the building, which is more than a hundred years old and was originally designed as a military barracks, making it difficult if not impossible to convert it properly at a reasonable cost, is clearly not suitable for accommodating children;

‐ the size of the institution, which accommodates more than 500 children and adults, some of them suffering from various physical and mental handicaps, makes its closure risky;

‐ the institution seems poorly provided with human resources, and their training is not appropriate to the requirements and needs of the children in the actual situation.

The Commission will be contacting the Romanian authorities to consider possible solutions, the most logical of which would be the closure of the institution and the readmission of the children to various other establishments in the region.

***

Question No 112 by Robin Teverson (H−0589/97)

Subject: Freezing of 5b budget funding in Devon and Cornwall, SW of United Kingdom

Further to the freezing of the South West of England Objetive 5b programme earlier this year, does the Commission expect the programme to be unfrozen?

Answer

The Community funds for the South West England Objective 5b Programme are not frozen. At the Monitoring Committee on 4 December 1996 it was agreed that a stop to the approval of all projects under Priorities 1 (SME and Business Development) and 2 (Tourism) of the programme should be made in light of the need to consider the findings of the interim Evaluation and to consider the scope for large‐scale projects. At the most recent meeting of the Monitoring Committee dated 30 May 1997, it was agreed that offer letters could be issued to those projects where there were no issues outstanding and which were in the Priority 1 and Priority 2 'Approvals Pending' category. Since this meeting, the programme Secretariat has been given discretion to issue offer letters to projects from the Priority 1 'Preparation Pool'. The way forward for the remaining years of the programme will be discussed in full at the next meeting of the Monitoring Committee scheduled for 30 September 1997, when the recommendations of the Interim Evaluation exercise will be considered in detail.

Question No 113 by Mark Watts (H−0590/97)

Subject: Subsidies for tobacco industry

Is the Commission aware that the European Union is spending almost 900 million ECU each year on subsidies for the tobacco industry?

Does the Commission agree that it is a grotesque contradiction for the EU to be spending 40 times more money on subsidizing tobacco than it does on anti‐cancer programmes?

Will the Commission now take steps to eliminate direct income aids for the tobacco industry?

Answer

The Commission can confirm that the Community spends about 900 million ECU each year on subsidies to tobacco growers.

If all raw tobacco production was to be stopped, the industry would obtain supplies from non‐member countries. Even now about 70 % of tobacco used in the Community for cigarette production is imported from third countries.

Moreover, the Community has created a Fund which covers research for the least harmful varieties, health information and education. This Fund is financed by the deduction of up to 1 % from the premium given to the tobacco producers, and the Commission proposes to double this amount.

***

Question No 114 by Hugh McMahon (H−0332/97)

Subject: Philoxenia programme

What progress has been made in developing the Commission's Philoxenia programme?

Answer

The proposal for a First multiannual programme to Assist European Tourism – 'Philoxenia' (1997‐2000), was adopted by the Commission on 30 April 1996 and presented to the Council and to the European Parliament. The European Parliament, the Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions have each given an Opinion on the proposal during the course of 1996.

The proposal was examined at several meetings of working groups of the Council during the course of 1996, and had been due to be considered at a meeting of the Council of Ministers planned for December 1996. This meeting was postponed by the President of the Council following indications that the unanimity required for the adoption of the proposal would not be forthcoming in the Council. There have been no subsequent discussions of the proposal at the level of the Council during the first semester of 1997 under the Dutch Presidency. With a view to progress the examination of the Philoxenia programme, the Presidency of the Council has been encouraged to resume discussions on the proposal. The Commission will continue to deploy its efforts over the coming months with a view to reaching a satisfactory conclusion.
